<p>The <b>Hubble Space Telescope</b> (<b>HST</b>) is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_observatory" title="Space observatory">space telescope</a> that was launched into <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> in 1990 and remains in operation. Although not the <a href="/wiki/Orbiting_Astronomical_Observatory_2" title="Orbiting Astronomical Observatory 2">first space telescope</a>, Hubble is one of the largest and most versatile, and is well known as both a vital research tool and a public relations boon for <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>. The HST is named after the <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomer</a> <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a>, and is one of NASA's <a href="/wiki/Great_Observatories_program" title="Great Observatories program">Great Observatories</a>, along with the <a href="/wiki/Compton_Gamma_Ray_Observatory" title="Compton Gamma Ray Observatory">Compton Gamma Ray Observatory</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory" title="Chandra X-ray Observatory">Chandra X-ray Observatory</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer Space Telescope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>With a 2.4-meter (7.9 ft) mirror, Hubble's four main instruments observe in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near_ultraviolet" title="Near ultraviolet">near ultraviolet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near_infrared" title="Near infrared">near infrared</a> <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">spectra</a>. Hubble's orbit outside the distortion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a> allows it to take extremely high-resolution images, with substantially lower <a href="/wiki/Background_(astronomy)" title="Background (astronomy)">background</a> light than ground-based telescopes. Hubble has recorded some of the most detailed <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible light</a> images ever, allowing a deep view into space and time. Many Hubble observations have led to breakthroughs in <a href="/wiki/Astrophysics" title="Astrophysics">astrophysics</a>, such as accurately <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law#Using_Hubble_space_telescope_data" title="Hubble's law">determining the rate of expansion of the universe</a>.</p>
<p>The HST was built by the United States space agency <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>, with contributions from the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Space_Telescope_Science_Institute" title="Space Telescope Science Institute">Space Telescope Science Institute</a> (STScI) selects Hubble's targets and processes the resulting data, while the <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">Goddard Space Flight Center</a> controls the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Space telescopes were proposed as early as 1923. Hubble was funded in the 1970s, with a proposed launch in 1983, but the project was beset by technical delays, budget problems, and the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster"><i>Challenger</i> disaster</a> (1986). When finally launched in 1990, Hubble's main mirror was found to have been ground incorrectly, compromising the telescope's capabilities. The optics were corrected to their intended quality by a <a href="/wiki/STS-61" title="STS-61">servicing mission</a> in 1993.</p>
<p>Hubble is the only telescope designed to be serviced in space by <a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">astronauts</a>. After launch by <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a></span> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Discovery" title="Space Shuttle Discovery"><i>Discovery</i></a> in 1990, five subsequent Space Shuttle missions repaired, upgraded, and replaced systems on the telescope, including all five of the main instruments. The fifth mission was initially canceled on safety grounds following the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Columbia disaster"><i>Columbia</i> disaster</a> (2003). However, after spirited public discussion, NASA administrator <a href="/wiki/Michael_D._Griffin" title="Michael D. Griffin">Mike Griffin</a> approved the <a href="/wiki/STS-125" title="STS-125">fifth servicing mission</a>, completed in 2009. The telescope is operating as of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, and could last until 2030–2040.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbsnews20130530_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-cbsnews20130530-3">[3]</a></sup> Its scientific successor, the <a href="/wiki/James_Webb_Space_Telescope" title="James Webb Space Telescope">James Webb Space Telescope</a> (JWST), is scheduled for launch in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span id="Conception,_design_and_aim"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Conception.2C_design_and_aim">Conception, design and aim</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Conception, design and aim">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposals_and_precursors">Proposals and precursors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Proposals and precursors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2939" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg/220px-Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg/330px-Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg/440px-Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Astronaut Owen Garriott works next to Skylab's manned solar space observatory, 1973</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1923, <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Oberth" title="Hermann Oberth">Hermann Oberth</a>—considered a father of modern rocketry, along with <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Goddard" title="Robert H. Goddard">Robert H. Goddard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Tsiolkovsky" title="Konstantin Tsiolkovsky">Konstantin Tsiolkovsky</a>—published <i><span lang="de">Die Rakete zu den Planetenräumen</span></i> ("The Rocket into Planetary Space"), which mentioned how a telescope could be propelled into Earth orbit by a rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The history of the Hubble Space Telescope can be traced back as far as 1946, to the <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomer</a> <a href="/wiki/Lyman_Spitzer" title="Lyman Spitzer">Lyman Spitzer</a>'s paper "Astronomical advantages of an extraterrestrial observatory".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> In it, he discussed the two main advantages that a space-based observatory would have over ground-based telescopes. First, the <a href="/wiki/Angular_resolution" title="Angular resolution">angular resolution</a> (the smallest separation at which objects can be clearly distinguished) would be limited only by <a href="/wiki/Diffraction-limited_system" title="Diffraction-limited system">diffraction</a>, rather than by the turbulence in the atmosphere, which causes stars to twinkle, known to astronomers as <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_seeing" title="Astronomical seeing">seeing</a>. At that time ground-based telescopes were limited to resolutions of 0.5–1.0 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcsecond" title="Arcsecond">arcseconds</a>, compared to a theoretical diffraction-limited resolution of about 0.05 arcsec for a telescope with a <a href="/wiki/Mirror" title="Mirror">mirror</a> 2.5 m in diameter. Second, a space-based telescope could observe <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light, which are strongly absorbed by the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>.</p>
<p>Spitzer devoted much of his career to pushing for the development of a space telescope. In 1962, a report by the US <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a> recommended the development of a <a href="/wiki/Space_telescope" title="Space telescope">space telescope</a> as part of the <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">space program</a>, and in 1965 Spitzer was appointed as head of a committee given the task of defining scientific objectives for a large space telescope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Space-based astronomy had begun on a very small scale following <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, as scientists made use of developments that had taken place in <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocket</a> technology. The first ultraviolet <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">spectrum</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> was obtained in 1946,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">National Aeronautics and Space Administration</a> (NASA) launched the <a href="/wiki/Orbiting_Solar_Observatory" title="Orbiting Solar Observatory">Orbiting Solar Observatory</a> (OSO) to obtain UV, X-ray, and gamma-ray spectra in 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Ariel_1" title="Ariel 1">orbiting solar telescope</a> was launched in 1962 by the United Kingdom as part of the <a href="/wiki/Ariel_programme" title="Ariel programme">Ariel space program</a>, and in 1966 NASA launched the first <a href="/wiki/Orbiting_Astronomical_Observatory" title="Orbiting Astronomical Observatory">Orbiting Astronomical Observatory</a> (OAO) mission. OAO-1's battery failed after three days, terminating the mission. It was followed by <a href="/wiki/Orbiting_Astronomical_Observatory_2" title="Orbiting Astronomical Observatory 2">OAO-2</a>, which carried out ultraviolet observations of <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a> from its launch in 1968 until 1972, well beyond its original planned lifetime of one year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The OSO and OAO missions demonstrated the important role space-based observations could play in astronomy, and in 1968, NASA developed firm plans for a space-based <a href="/wiki/Reflecting_telescope" title="Reflecting telescope">reflecting telescope</a> with a mirror 3 m in diameter, known provisionally as the Large Orbiting Telescope or Large Space Telescope (LST), with a launch slated for 1979. These plans emphasized the need for manned maintenance missions to the telescope to ensure such a costly program had a lengthy working life, and the concurrent development of plans for the reusable <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">space shuttle</a> indicated that the technology to allow this was soon to become available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quest_for_funding">Quest for funding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Quest for funding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The continuing success of the OAO program encouraged increasingly strong consensus within the astronomical community that the LST should be a major goal. In 1970, NASA established two committees, one to plan the engineering side of the space telescope project, and the other to determine the scientific goals of the mission. Once these had been established, the next hurdle for NASA was to obtain funding for the instrument, which would be far more costly than any Earth-based telescope. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">U.S. Congress</a> questioned many aspects of the proposed budget for the telescope and forced cuts in the budget for the planning stages, which at the time consisted of very detailed studies of potential instruments and hardware for the telescope. In 1974, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_spending" title="Public spending">public spending</a> cuts led to Congress deleting all funding for the telescope project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response to this, a nationwide lobbying effort was coordinated among astronomers. Many astronomers met <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">congressmen</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">senators</a> in person, and large scale letter-writing campaigns were organized. The National Academy of Sciences published a report emphasizing the need for a space telescope, and eventually the Senate agreed to half of the budget that had originally been approved by Congress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telescope34_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-telescope34-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The funding issues led to something of a reduction in the scale of the project, with the proposed mirror diameter reduced from 3 m to 2.4 m, both to cut costs <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gander_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-gander-18">[18]</a></sup> and to allow a more compact and effective configuration for the telescope hardware. A proposed precursor 1.5 m space telescope to test the systems to be used on the main satellite was dropped, and budgetary concerns also prompted collaboration with the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>. ESA agreed to provide funding and supply one of the first generation instruments for the telescope, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a> that would power it, and staff to work on the telescope in the United States, in return for European astronomers being guaranteed at least 15% of the observing time on the telescope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Congress eventually approved funding of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$36 million</span> for 1978, and the design of the LST began in earnest, aiming for a launch date of 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telescope34_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-telescope34-17">[17]</a></sup> In 1983 the telescope was named after <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> who made one of the greatest scientific breakthroughs of the 20th century when he discovered that the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expanding_universe" title="Expanding universe">expanding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction_and_engineering">Construction and engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Construction and engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_mirror_polishing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2401" data-file-width="3000" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Hubble_mirror_polishing.jpg/220px-Hubble_mirror_polishing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Hubble_mirror_polishing.jpg/330px-Hubble_mirror_polishing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Hubble_mirror_polishing.jpg/440px-Hubble_mirror_polishing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_mirror_polishing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grinding of Hubble's primary mirror at Perkin-Elmer, March 1979</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Once the Space Telescope project had been given the go-ahead, work on the program was divided among many institutions. <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall Space Flight Center</a> (MSFC) was given responsibility for the design, development, and construction of the telescope, while <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">Goddard Space Flight Center</a> was given overall control of the scientific instruments and ground-control center for the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> MSFC commissioned the optics company <a href="/wiki/PerkinElmer" title="PerkinElmer">Perkin-Elmer</a> to design and build the Optical Telescope Assembly (OTA) and Fine Guidance Sensors for the space telescope. <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Corporation" title="Lockheed Corporation">Lockheed</a> was commissioned to construct and integrate the spacecraft in which the telescope would be housed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunar.2C_p._489_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunar.2C_p._489-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Optical_Telescope_Assembly_(OTA)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Optical_Telescope_Assembly_.28OTA.29">Optical Telescope Assembly (OTA)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Optical Telescope Assembly (OTA)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Optically, the HST is a <a href="/wiki/Cassegrain_reflector" title="Cassegrain reflector">Cassegrain reflector</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ritchey%E2%80%93Chr%C3%A9tien_telescope" title="Ritchey–Chrétien telescope">Ritchey–Chrétien design</a>, as are most large professional telescopes. This design, with two hyperbolic mirrors, is known for good imaging performance over a wide field of view, with the disadvantage that the mirrors have shapes that are hard to fabricate and test. The mirror and optical systems of the telescope determine the final performance, and they were designed to exacting specifications. Optical telescopes typically have mirrors polished to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accuracy" title="Accuracy">accuracy</a> of about a tenth of the <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible light</a>, but the Space Telescope was to be used for observations from the visible through the ultraviolet (shorter wavelengths) and was specified to be <a href="/wiki/Diffraction-limited_system" title="Diffraction-limited system">diffraction limited</a> to take full advantage of the space environment. Therefore, its mirror needed to be polished to an accuracy of 10 <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">nanometers</a>, or about 1/65 of the wavelength of red light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceSPF_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceSPF-24">[24]</a></sup> On the long wavelength end, the OTA was not designed with optimum IR performance in mind—for example, the mirrors are kept at stable (and warm, about 15 °C) temperatures by heaters. This limits Hubble's performance as an infrared telescope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IR_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-IR-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_backup_mirror.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="2848" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Hubble_backup_mirror.jpg/170px-Hubble_backup_mirror.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Hubble_backup_mirror.jpg/255px-Hubble_backup_mirror.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Hubble_backup_mirror.jpg/340px-Hubble_backup_mirror.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_backup_mirror.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The backup mirror, by Kodak; its inner support structure can be seen because it is not coated with a reflective surface</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Perkin-Elmer intended to use custom-built and extremely sophisticated <a href="/wiki/Computer-aided_manufacturing" title="Computer-aided manufacturing">computer-controlled polishing machines</a> to grind the mirror to the required shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunar.2C_p._489_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dunar.2C_p._489-23">[23]</a></sup> However, in case their cutting-edge technology ran into difficulties, NASA demanded that PE sub-contract to <a href="/wiki/Kodak" title="Kodak">Kodak</a> to construct a back-up mirror using traditional mirror-polishing techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> (The team of Kodak and <a href="/wiki/Itek" title="Itek">Itek</a> also bid on the original mirror polishing work. Their bid called for the two companies to double-check each other's work, which would have almost certainly caught the polishing error that later caused <a href="#Flawed_mirror">such problems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>) The Kodak mirror is now on permanent display at the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> An Itek mirror built as part of the effort is now used in the 2.4 m telescope at the <a href="/wiki/Magdalena_Ridge_Observatory" title="Magdalena Ridge Observatory">Magdalena Ridge Observatory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Construction of the Perkin-Elmer mirror began in 1979, starting with a blank manufactured by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corning_Incorporated" title="Corning Incorporated">Corning</a> from their ultra-low expansion <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a>. To keep the mirror's weight to a minimum it consisted of top and bottom plates, each one inch (25.4 mm) thick, sandwiching a <a href="/wiki/Honeycomb" title="Honeycomb">honeycomb</a> lattice. Perkin-Elmer simulated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microgravity" title="Microgravity">microgravity</a> by supporting the mirror from the back with 130 rods that exerted varying amounts of force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> This ensured that the mirror's final shape would be correct and to specification when finally deployed. Mirror polishing continued until May 1981. NASA reports at the time questioned Perkin-Elmer's managerial structure, and the polishing began to slip behind schedule and over budget. To save money, NASA halted work on the back-up mirror and put the launch date of the telescope back to October 1984.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The mirror was completed by the end of 1981; it was washed using 2,400 gallons (9,100 L) of hot, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deionized_water" title="Deionized water">deionized water</a> and then received a reflective coating of 65 nm-thick <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminum</a> and a protective coating of 25 nm-thick <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_fluoride" title="Magnesium fluoride">magnesium fluoride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IR_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-IR-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Early_stages_of_Hubble_construction.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="800" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Early_stages_of_Hubble_construction.jpg/220px-Early_stages_of_Hubble_construction.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Early_stages_of_Hubble_construction.jpg/330px-Early_stages_of_Hubble_construction.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Early_stages_of_Hubble_construction.jpg/440px-Early_stages_of_Hubble_construction.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Early_stages_of_Hubble_construction.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The OTA, metering truss, and secondary baffle are visible in this image of Hubble during early construction.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Doubts continued to be expressed about Perkin-Elmer's competence on a project of this importance, as their budget and timescale for producing the rest of the OTA continued to inflate. In response to a schedule described as "unsettled and changing daily", NASA postponed the launch date of the telescope until April 1985. Perkin-Elmer's schedules continued to slip at a rate of about one month per quarter, and at times delays reached one day for each day of work. NASA was forced to postpone the launch date until March and then September 1986. By this time, the total project budget had risen to <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$1.175 billion</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_systems">Spacecraft systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Spacecraft systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The spacecraft in which the telescope and instruments were to be housed was another major engineering challenge. It would have to withstand frequent passages from direct sunlight into the darkness of Earth's <a href="/wiki/Shadow" title="Shadow">shadow</a>, which would cause major changes in temperature, while being stable enough to allow extremely accurate pointing of the telescope. A shroud of <a href="/wiki/Multi-layer_insulation" title="Multi-layer insulation">multi-layer insulation</a> keeps the temperature within the telescope stable and surrounds a light aluminum shell in which the telescope and instruments sit. Within the shell, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fiber_reinforced_plastic" title="Carbon fiber reinforced plastic">graphite-epoxy</a> frame keeps the working parts of the telescope firmly aligned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Because graphite composites are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" title="Hygroscopic">hygroscopic</a>, there was a risk that water vapor absorbed by the truss while in Lockheed's clean room would later be expressed in the vacuum of space; resulting in the telescope's instruments being covered by ice. To reduce that risk, a nitrogen gas purge was performed before launching the telescope into space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>While construction of the spacecraft in which the telescope and instruments would be housed proceeded somewhat more smoothly than the construction of the OTA, Lockheed still experienced some budget and schedule slippage, and by the summer of 1985, construction of the spacecraft was 30% over budget and three months behind schedule. An MSFC report said that Lockheed tended to rely on NASA directions rather than take their own initiative in the construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Computer_systems_and_data_processing">Computer systems and data processing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Computer systems and data processing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DF-224.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="768" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/DF-224.jpg/220px-DF-224.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/DF-224.jpg/330px-DF-224.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/DF-224.jpg/440px-DF-224.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DF-224.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
DF-224 in Hubble, before it was replaced in 1999</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The two initial, primary computers on the HST were the 1.25 MHz <a href="/wiki/DF-224" title="DF-224">DF-224</a> system, built by Rockwell Autonetics, which contained three redundant CPUs, and two redundant <a href="/wiki/NSSC-1" title="NSSC-1">NSSC-1</a> (NASA Standard Spacecraft Computer, Model 1) systems, developed by Westinghouse and GSFC using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diode-transistor_logic" title="Diode-transistor logic">diode-transistor logic</a> (DTL). A co-processor for the DF-224 was added during Servicing Mission 1 in 1993, which consisted of two redundant strings of an Intel-based 80386 processor with an 80387 math co-processor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The DF-224 and its 386 co-processor were replaced by a 25 MHz Intel-based 80486 processor system during Servicing Mission 3A in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additionally, some of the science instruments and components had their own embedded microprocessor-based control systems. The MATs (Multiple Access Transponder) components, MAT-1 and MAT-2, utilize Hughes Aircraft CDP1802CD microprocessors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The Wide Field and Planetary Camera (WFPC) also utilized an RCA 1802 microprocessor (or possibly the older 1801 version).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The WFPC-1 was replaced by the WFPC-2 during Servicing Mission 1 in 1993, which was then replaced by the Wide Field Camera 3 (WFC3) during Servicing Mission 4 in 2009.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_instruments">Initial instruments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Initial instruments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HubbleExploded_edit_1.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="1100" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/HubbleExploded_edit_1.svg/260px-HubbleExploded_edit_1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/HubbleExploded_edit_1.svg/390px-HubbleExploded_edit_1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/HubbleExploded_edit_1.svg/520px-HubbleExploded_edit_1.svg.png 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HubbleExploded_edit_1.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exploded_view" title="Exploded view">Exploded view</a> of the Hubble Space Telescope</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Wide_Field_and_Planetary_Camera" title="Wide Field and Planetary Camera">Wide Field and Planetary Camera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goddard_High_Resolution_Spectrograph" title="Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph">Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph</a>, <a href="/wiki/High_Speed_Photometer" title="High Speed Photometer">High Speed Photometer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faint_Object_Camera" title="Faint Object Camera">Faint Object Camera</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Faint_Object_Spectrograph" title="Faint Object Spectrograph">Faint Object Spectrograph</a></div>
<p>When launched, the HST carried five scientific instruments: the Wide Field and Planetary Camera (WF/PC), Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph (GHRS), High Speed Photometer (HSP), Faint Object Camera (FOC) and the Faint Object Spectrograph (FOS). WF/PC was a high-resolution imaging device primarily intended for optical observations. It was built by NASA's <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a>, and incorporated a set of 48 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filter_(optics)" title="Filter (optics)">filters</a> isolating <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">spectral lines</a> of particular astrophysical interest. The instrument contained eight <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">charge-coupled device</a> (CCD) chips divided between two cameras, each using four CCDs. Each CCD has a resolution of 0.64 megapixels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The "wide field camera" (WFC) covered a large angular field at the expense of resolution, while the "planetary camera" (PC) took images at a longer effective <a href="/wiki/Focal_length" title="Focal length">focal length</a> than the WF chips, giving it a greater magnification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CP-2244_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-CP-2244-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The GHRS was a <a href="/wiki/Spectrograph" title="Spectrograph">spectrograph</a> designed to operate in the ultraviolet. It was built by the Goddard Space Flight Center and could achieve a <a href="/wiki/Spectral_resolution" title="Spectral resolution">spectral resolution</a> of 90,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Also optimized for ultraviolet observations were the FOC and FOS, which were capable of the highest spatial resolution of any instruments on Hubble. Rather than CCDs these three instruments used <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a>-counting <a href="/wiki/Digicon" title="Digicon">digicons</a> as their detectors. The FOC was constructed by ESA, while the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_San_Diego" title="University of California, San Diego">University of California, San Diego</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_Marietta_Corporation" title="Martin Marietta Corporation">Martin Marietta Corporation</a> built the FOS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CP-2244_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-CP-2244-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The final instrument was the HSP, designed and built at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison" title="University of Wisconsin–Madison">University of Wisconsin–Madison</a>. It was optimized for visible and ultraviolet light observations of <a href="/wiki/Variable_star" title="Variable star">variable stars</a> and other astronomical objects varying in brightness. It could take up to 100,000 measurements per second with a <a href="/wiki/Photometry_(astronomy)" title="Photometry (astronomy)">photometric</a> accuracy of about 2% or better.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>HST's guidance system can also be used as a scientific instrument. Its three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fine_Guidance_Sensor" title="Fine Guidance Sensor">Fine Guidance Sensors</a> (FGS) are primarily used to keep the telescope accurately pointed during an observation, but can also be used to carry out extremely accurate <a href="/wiki/Astrometry" title="Astrometry">astrometry</a>; measurements accurate to within 0.0003 arcseconds have been achieved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ground_support">Ground support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ground support">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_Control_Centre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Hubble_Control_Centre.jpg/220px-Hubble_Control_Centre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Hubble_Control_Centre.jpg/330px-Hubble_Control_Centre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Hubble_Control_Centre.jpg/440px-Hubble_Control_Centre.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_Control_Centre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hubble Control Center at Goddard Space Flight Center, 1999</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Telescope_Science_Institute" title="Space Telescope Science Institute">Space Telescope Science Institute</a></div>
<p>The Space Telescope Science Institute (STScI) is responsible for the scientific operation of the telescope and the delivery of data products to astronomers. STScI is operated by the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Universities_for_Research_in_Astronomy" title="Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy">Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy</a> (AURA) and is physically located in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore,_Maryland" title="Baltimore, Maryland">Baltimore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a> on the Homewood campus of <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins University</a>, one of the 39 US universities and seven international affiliates that make up the AURA consortium. STScI was established in 1981<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edmondson1997_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edmondson1997-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> after something of a power struggle between NASA and the scientific community at large. NASA had wanted to keep this function in-house, but scientists wanted it to be based in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academia" title="Academia">academic</a> establishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Space_Telescope_European_Coordinating_Facility" title="Space Telescope European Coordinating Facility">Space Telescope European Coordinating Facility</a> (ST-ECF), established at <a href="/wiki/Garching_bei_M%C3%BCnchen" title="Garching bei München">Garching bei München</a> near <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> in 1984, provided similar support for European astronomers until 2011, when these activities were moved to the European Space Astronomy Centre.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_of_Hubble%27s_orbit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="933" data-file-width="1049" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Diagram_of_Hubble%27s_orbit.jpg/220px-Diagram_of_Hubble%27s_orbit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Diagram_of_Hubble%27s_orbit.jpg/330px-Diagram_of_Hubble%27s_orbit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Diagram_of_Hubble%27s_orbit.jpg/440px-Diagram_of_Hubble%27s_orbit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_of_Hubble%27s_orbit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hubble's low orbit means many targets are visible for somewhat less than half of an orbit's elapsed time, since they are blocked from view by the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> for one-half of each orbit.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One rather complex task that falls to STScI is scheduling observations for the telescope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Team_Hubble_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Team_Hubble-51">[51]</a></sup> Hubble is in a low-Earth orbit to enable servicing missions, but this means that most astronomical targets are <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occulted</a> by the Earth for slightly less than half of each orbit. Observations cannot take place when the telescope passes through the <a href="/wiki/South_Atlantic_Anomaly" title="South Atlantic Anomaly">South Atlantic Anomaly</a> due to elevated <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> levels, and there are also sizable exclusion zones around the Sun (precluding observations of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>), Moon and Earth. The solar avoidance angle is about 50°, to keep sunlight from illuminating any part of the OTA. Earth and Moon avoidance keeps bright light out of the FGSs, and keeps scattered light from entering the instruments. If the FGSs are turned off, however, the Moon and Earth can be observed. Earth observations were used very early in the program to generate flat-fields for the WFPC1 instrument. There is a so-called continuous viewing zone (CVZ), at roughly 90° to the plane of Hubble's orbit, in which targets are not <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occulted</a> for long periods. Due to the <a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">precession</a> of the orbit, the location of the CVZ moves slowly over a period of eight weeks. Because the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Limb" title="wikt:Limb">limb</a> of the Earth is always within about 30° of regions within the CVZ, the brightness of scattered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earthshine" title="Earthshine">earthshine</a> may be elevated for long periods during CVZ observations.</p>
<p>Hubble orbits in the upper atmosphere at an altitude of approximately 547 kilometers (340 mi) and an inclination of 28.5°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubblesite-facts_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-hubblesite-facts-1">[1]</a></sup> The position along its orbit changes over time in a way that is not accurately predictable. The density of the upper atmosphere varies according to many factors, and this means that Hubble's predicted position for six weeks' time could be in error by up to 4,000 km (2,500 mi). Observation schedules are typically finalized only a few days in advance, as a longer lead time would mean there was a chance that the target would be unobservable by the time it was due to be observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Engineering support for HST is provided by NASA and contractor personnel at the <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">Goddard Space Flight Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greenbelt,_Maryland" title="Greenbelt, Maryland">Greenbelt, Maryland</a>, 48 km (30 mi) south of the STScI. Hubble's operation is monitored 24 hours per day by four teams of flight controllers who make up Hubble's Flight Operations Team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Team_Hubble_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Team_Hubble-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Challenger_disaster,_delays,_and_eventual_launch"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Challenger_disaster.2C_delays.2C_and_eventual_launch"><i>Challenger</i> disaster, delays, and eventual launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Challenger disaster, delays, and eventual launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS-31_Hubble_launch_roll_and_pitch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2400" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/STS-31_Hubble_launch_roll_and_pitch.jpg/170px-STS-31_Hubble_launch_roll_and_pitch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/STS-31_Hubble_launch_roll_and_pitch.jpg/255px-STS-31_Hubble_launch_roll_and_pitch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/STS-31_Hubble_launch_roll_and_pitch.jpg/340px-STS-31_Hubble_launch_roll_and_pitch.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS-31_Hubble_launch_roll_and_pitch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/STS-31" title="STS-31">STS-31</a> lifts off, carrying Hubble into orbit.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By early 1986, the planned launch date of October that year looked feasible, but the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster"><i>Challenger</i> accident</a> brought the U.S. space program to a halt, grounding the Space Shuttle fleet and forcing the launch of Hubble to be postponed for several years. The telescope had to be kept in a clean room, powered up and purged with nitrogen, until a launch could be rescheduled. This costly situation (about <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$6 million</span> per month) pushed the overall costs of the project even higher. This delay did allow time for engineers to perform extensive tests, swap out a possibly failure-prone battery, and make other improvements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Furthermore, the ground software needed to control Hubble was not ready in 1986, and in fact was barely ready by the 1990 launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eventually, following the resumption of shuttle flights in 1988, the launch of the telescope was scheduled for 1990. On April 24, 1990, shuttle mission <a href="/wiki/STS-31" title="STS-31">STS-31</a> saw <i>Discovery</i> launch the telescope successfully into its planned orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>From its original total cost estimate of about <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$400 million</span>, the telescope cost about <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$4.7 billion</span> by the time of its launch. Hubble's cumulative costs were estimated to be about <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$10 billion</span> in 2010, twenty years after launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_Hubble_instruments">List of Hubble instruments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: List of Hubble instruments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hs-2009-25-e-full_jpg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2908" data-file-width="2439" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hs-2009-25-e-full_jpg.jpg/170px-Hs-2009-25-e-full_jpg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hs-2009-25-e-full_jpg.jpg/255px-Hs-2009-25-e-full_jpg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hs-2009-25-e-full_jpg.jpg/340px-Hs-2009-25-e-full_jpg.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hs-2009-25-e-full_jpg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Some of the Carina nebula by WFC3</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hubble accommodates five science instruments at a given time, plus the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fine_Guidance_Sensor" title="Fine Guidance Sensor">Fine Guidance Sensors</a>, which are mainly used for aiming the telescope but are occasionally used for science (<a href="/wiki/Astrometry" title="Astrometry">astrometry</a>). Early instruments were replaced with more advanced ones during the Shuttle servicing missions. COSTAR was strictly a corrective optics device rather than a true science instrument, but occupied one of the five instrument bays.</p>
<p>Since the final servicing mission in 2009, the four active instruments have been ACS, COS, STIS and WFC3. NICMOS is kept in hibernation, but may be revived if WFC3 were to fail in the future.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Advanced_Camera_for_Surveys" title="Advanced Camera for Surveys">Advanced Camera for Surveys</a> (ACS; 2002–present)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cosmic_Origins_Spectrograph" title="Cosmic Origins Spectrograph">Cosmic Origins Spectrograph</a> (COS; 2009–present)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Corrective_Optics_Space_Telescope_Axial_Replacement" title="Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement">Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement</a> (COSTAR; 1993–2009)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Faint_Object_Camera" title="Faint Object Camera">Faint Object Camera</a> (FOC; 1990–2002)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Faint_Object_Spectrograph" title="Faint Object Spectrograph">Faint Object Spectrograph</a> (FOS; 1990–1997)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fine_Guidance_Sensor" title="Fine Guidance Sensor">Fine Guidance Sensor</a> (FGS; 1990–present)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Goddard_High_Resolution_Spectrograph" title="Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph">Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph</a> (GHRS/HRS; 1990–1997)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/High_Speed_Photometer" title="High Speed Photometer">High Speed Photometer</a> (HSP; 1990–1993)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Near_Infrared_Camera_and_Multi-Object_Spectrometer" title="Near Infrared Camera and Multi-Object Spectrometer">Near Infrared Camera and Multi-Object Spectrometer</a> (NICMOS; 1997–present, hibernating since 2008)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Space_Telescope_Imaging_Spectrograph" title="Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph">Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph</a> (STIS; 1997–present (non-operative 2004–2009)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wide_Field_and_Planetary_Camera" title="Wide Field and Planetary Camera">Wide Field and Planetary Camera</a> (WFPC; 1990–1993)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wide_Field_and_Planetary_Camera_2" title="Wide Field and Planetary Camera 2">Wide Field and Planetary Camera 2</a> (WFPC2; 1993–2009)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wide_Field_Camera_3" title="Wide Field Camera 3">Wide Field Camera 3</a> (WFC3; 2009–present)</li>
</ul>
<p>Of the former instruments, three (COSTAR, FOS and WFPC2) are displayed in the Smithsonian <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a>. The FOC is in the Dornier museum, Germany. The HSP is in the Space Place at the University of Wisconsin-Madison. The first WFPC was dismantled, and some components were then re-used in WFC3. The current location of GHRS is unclear.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flawed_mirror">Flawed mirror</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Flawed mirror">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Within weeks of the launch of the telescope, the returned images indicated a serious problem with the optical system. Although the first images appeared to be sharper than those of ground-based telescopes, Hubble failed to achieve a final sharp focus and the best image quality obtained was drastically lower than expected. Images of <a href="/wiki/Point_source" title="Point source">point sources</a> spread out over a radius of more than one arcsecond, instead of having a <a href="/wiki/Point_spread_function" title="Point spread function">point spread function</a> (PSF) concentrated within a circle 0.1 arcsec in diameter as had been specified in the design criteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1990_s31_IMAX_view_of_HST_release.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1105" data-file-width="1730" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/1990_s31_IMAX_view_of_HST_release.jpg/220px-1990_s31_IMAX_view_of_HST_release.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/1990_s31_IMAX_view_of_HST_release.jpg/330px-1990_s31_IMAX_view_of_HST_release.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/1990_s31_IMAX_view_of_HST_release.jpg/440px-1990_s31_IMAX_view_of_HST_release.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1990_s31_IMAX_view_of_HST_release.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hubble being deployed from <i>Discovery</i> in 1990.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Analysis of the flawed images showed that the cause of the problem was that the primary mirror had been polished to the wrong shape. Although it was probably the most precisely figured optical mirror ever made, smooth to about 10 nanometers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceSPF_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceSPF-24">[24]</a></sup> at the perimeter it was too flat by about 2,200 nanometers (2.2 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">micrometers</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Servicing_Mission_1_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Servicing_Mission_1-59">[59]</a></sup> This difference was catastrophic, introducing severe <a href="/wiki/Spherical_aberration" title="Spherical aberration">spherical aberration</a>, a flaw in which light reflecting off the edge of a mirror <a href="/wiki/Focus_(optics)" title="Focus (optics)">focuses</a> on a different point from the light reflecting off its center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tatarewicz.2C_SP-4219.2C_p._375_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tatarewicz.2C_SP-4219.2C_p._375-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>The effect of the mirror flaw on scientific observations depended on the particular observation—the core of the aberrated PSF was sharp enough to permit high-resolution observations of bright objects, and spectroscopy of point sources was only affected through a sensitivity loss. However, the loss of light to the large, out of focus halo severely reduced the usefulness of the telescope for faint objects or high-contrast imaging. This meant that nearly all of the cosmological programs were essentially impossible, since they required observation of exceptionally faint objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tatarewicz.2C_SP-4219.2C_p._375_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tatarewicz.2C_SP-4219.2C_p._375-60">[60]</a></sup> NASA and the telescope became the butt of many jokes, and the project was popularly regarded as a <a href="/wiki/White_elephant" title="White elephant">white elephant</a>. For instance, in the 1991 comedy <i><a href="/wiki/The_Naked_Gun_2%C2%BD:_The_Smell_of_Fear" title="The Naked Gun 2½: The Smell of Fear">The Naked Gun 2½: The Smell of Fear</a></i>, Hubble was pictured with the <i><a href="/wiki/RMS_Lusitania" title="RMS Lusitania">Lusitania</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/LZ_129_Hindenburg" title="LZ 129 Hindenburg">Hindenburg</a></i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Edsel" title="Edsel">Edsel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Nonetheless, during the first three years of the Hubble mission, before the optical corrections, the telescope still carried out a large number of productive observations of less demanding targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The error was well characterized and stable, enabling astronomers to partially compensate for the defective mirror by using sophisticated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Image_processing" title="Image processing">image processing</a> techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Deconvolution" title="Deconvolution">deconvolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_the_problem">Origin of the problem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Origin of the problem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_PSF_with_flawed_optics.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="310" data-file-width="320" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Hubble_PSF_with_flawed_optics.jpg/220px-Hubble_PSF_with_flawed_optics.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7d/Hubble_PSF_with_flawed_optics.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_PSF_with_flawed_optics.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An extract from a <a href="/wiki/Wide_Field_and_Planetary_Camera" title="Wide Field and Planetary Camera">WF/PC</a> image shows the light from a star spread over a wide area instead of being concentrated on a few pixels.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A commission headed by <a href="/wiki/Lew_Allen" title="Lew Allen">Lew Allen</a>, director of the <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a>, was established to determine how the error could have arisen. The Allen Commission found that the main <a href="/wiki/Null_corrector" title="Null corrector">null corrector</a>, a testing device used to achieve a properly shaped non-spherical mirror, had been incorrectly assembled—one lens was out of position by 1.3 mm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> During the initial grinding and polishing of the mirror, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perkin-Elmer" title="Perkin-Elmer">Perkin-Elmer</a> analyzed its surface with two conventional null correctors. However, for the final manufacturing step (<a href="/wiki/Figuring" title="Figuring">figuring</a>), they switched to a custom-built null corrector, designed explicitly to meet very strict tolerances. The incorrect assembly of the device resulted in the mirror being ground very precisely but to the wrong shape. There was one later opportunity to catch the error, since for technical reasons a few of the final tests needed to use the two conventional null correctors. These tests correctly reported <a href="/wiki/Spherical_aberration" title="Spherical aberration">spherical aberration</a>, but were dismissed since the reflective null corrector was considered more accurate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The commission blamed the failings primarily on Perkin-Elmer. Relations between NASA and the optics company had been severely strained during the telescope construction, due to frequent schedule slippage and cost overruns. NASA found that Perkin-Elmer did not review or supervise the mirror construction adequately, did not assign its best optical scientists to the project (as it had for the prototype), and in particular did not involve the optical designers in the construction and verification of the mirror. While the commission heavily criticized Perkin-Elmer for these managerial failings, NASA was also criticized for not picking up on the quality control shortcomings, such as relying totally on test results from a single instrument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_of_a_solution">Design of a solution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Design of a solution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_Images_of_M100_Before_and_After_Mirror_Repair_-_GPN-2002-000064.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1605" data-file-width="3000" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Hubble_Images_of_M100_Before_and_After_Mirror_Repair_-_GPN-2002-000064.jpg/260px-Hubble_Images_of_M100_Before_and_After_Mirror_Repair_-_GPN-2002-000064.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Hubble_Images_of_M100_Before_and_After_Mirror_Repair_-_GPN-2002-000064.jpg/390px-Hubble_Images_of_M100_Before_and_After_Mirror_Repair_-_GPN-2002-000064.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Hubble_Images_of_M100_Before_and_After_Mirror_Repair_-_GPN-2002-000064.jpg/520px-Hubble_Images_of_M100_Before_and_After_Mirror_Repair_-_GPN-2002-000064.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_Images_of_M100_Before_and_After_Mirror_Repair_-_GPN-2002-000064.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The core of spiral galaxy <a href="/wiki/Messier_100" title="Messier 100">M100</a>, imaged with Hubble before and after corrective optics.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The design of the telescope had always incorporated servicing missions, and astronomers immediately began to seek potential solutions to the problem that could be applied at the first servicing mission, scheduled for 1993. While Kodak had ground a back-up mirror for Hubble, it would have been impossible to replace the mirror in orbit, and too expensive and time-consuming to bring the telescope back to Earth for a refit. Instead, the fact that the mirror had been ground so precisely to the wrong shape led to the design of new optical components with exactly the same error but in the opposite sense, to be added to the telescope at the servicing mission, effectively acting as "<a href="/wiki/Glasses" title="Glasses">spectacles</a>" to correct the spherical aberration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first step was a precise characterization of the error in the main mirror. Working backwards from images of point sources, astronomers determined that the <a href="/wiki/Conic_constant" title="Conic constant">conic constant</a> of the mirror as built was <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">2999898610000000000♠</span>−1.013<span style="margin-left:.25em;">90</span><span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.0002</span>, instead of the intended −1.00230.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The same number was also derived by analyzing the null corrector used by Perkin-Elmer to figure the mirror, as well as by analyzing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interferogram" title="Interferogram">interferograms</a> obtained during ground testing of the mirror.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Feustel_moving_COSTAR.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Feustel_moving_COSTAR.jpg/220px-Feustel_moving_COSTAR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Feustel_moving_COSTAR.jpg/330px-Feustel_moving_COSTAR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Feustel_moving_COSTAR.jpg/440px-Feustel_moving_COSTAR.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Feustel_moving_COSTAR.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
COSTAR being removed in 2009</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because of the way the HST's instruments were designed, two different sets of correctors were required. The design of the <a href="/wiki/Wide_Field_and_Planetary_Camera_2" title="Wide Field and Planetary Camera 2">Wide Field and Planetary Camera 2</a>, already planned to replace the existing WF/PC, included relay mirrors to direct light onto the four separate <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">charge-coupled device</a> (CCD) chips making up its two cameras. An inverse error built into their surfaces could completely cancel the aberration of the primary. However, the other instruments lacked any intermediate surfaces that could be figured in this way, and so required an external correction device.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tatarewicz.2C_SP-4219.2C_p._376_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tatarewicz.2C_SP-4219.2C_p._376-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Corrective_Optics_Space_Telescope_Axial_Replacement" title="Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement">Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement</a> (COSTAR) system was designed to correct the spherical aberration for light focused at the FOC, FOS, and GHRS. It consists of two mirrors in the light path with one ground to correct the aberration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> To fit the COSTAR system onto the telescope, one of the other instruments had to be removed, and astronomers selected the <a href="/wiki/High_Speed_Photometer" title="High Speed Photometer">High Speed Photometer</a> to be sacrificed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tatarewicz.2C_SP-4219.2C_p._376_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tatarewicz.2C_SP-4219.2C_p._376-72">[72]</a></sup> By 2002, all of the original instruments requiring COSTAR had been replaced by instruments with their own corrective optics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COSTARNotNeeded_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-COSTARNotNeeded-74">[74]</a></sup> COSTAR was removed and returned to Earth in 2009 where it is exhibited at the National Air and Space Museum. The area previously used by COSTAR is now occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_Origins_Spectrograph" title="Cosmic Origins Spectrograph">Cosmic Origins Spectrograph</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SM4_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-SM4-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Servicing_missions_and_new_instruments">Servicing missions and new instruments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Servicing missions and new instruments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0 0 1em 1em; padding: 3px;">
<div class="timeline-wrapper"><map name="timeline_db0818b3999d4d7907b2e6eb33e7e6d9">
<area alt="Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph" coords="248,124,307,145" href="/wiki/Space_Telescope_Imaging_Spectrograph" shape="rect" title="Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph"/>
<area alt="Faint Object Spectrograph" coords="48,124,101,145" href="/wiki/Faint_Object_Spectrograph" shape="rect" title="Faint Object Spectrograph"/>
<area alt="Advanced Camera for Surveys" coords="287,93,340,114" href="/wiki/Advanced_Camera_for_Surveys" shape="rect" title="Advanced Camera for Surveys"/>
<area alt="Faint Object Camera" coords="87,93,140,114" href="/wiki/Faint_Object_Camera" shape="rect" title="Faint Object Camera"/>
<area alt="Cosmic Origins Spectrograph" coords="341,62,393,82" href="/wiki/Cosmic_Origins_Spectrograph" shape="rect" title="Cosmic Origins Spectrograph"/>
<area alt="Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement" coords="164,62,233,82" href="/wiki/Corrective_Optics_Space_Telescope_Axial_Replacement" shape="rect" title="Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement"/>
<area alt="High Speed Photometer" coords="18,62,70,82" href="/wiki/High_Speed_Photometer" shape="rect" title="High Speed Photometer"/>
<area alt="Near Infrared Camera and Multi-Object Spectrometer" coords="248,30,318,51" href="/wiki/Near_Infrared_Camera_and_Multi%2DObject_Spectrometer" shape="rect" title="Near Infrared Camera and Multi-Object Spectrometer"/>
<area alt="Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph" coords="48,30,107,51" href="/wiki/Goddard_High_Resolution_Spectrograph" shape="rect" title="Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph"/>
<area alt="Wide Field Camera 3" coords="341,-1,399,20" href="/wiki/Wide_Field_Camera_3" shape="rect" title="Wide Field Camera 3"/>
<area alt="Wide Field and Planetary Camera 2" coords="164,-1,228,20" href="/wiki/Wide_Field_and_Planetary_Camera_2" shape="rect" title="Wide Field and Planetary Camera 2"/>
<area alt="Wide Field and Planetary Camera" coords="18,-1,76,20" href="/wiki/Wide_Field_and_Planetary_Camera" shape="rect" title="Wide Field and Planetary Camera"/></map><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/timeline/db0818b3999d4d7907b2e6eb33e7e6d9.png" usemap="#timeline_db0818b3999d4d7907b2e6eb33e7e6d9"/></div>
</div>
<p>Hubble was designed to accommodate regular servicing and equipment upgrades while in orbit. Instruments and limited life items were designed as <a href="/wiki/Orbital_replacement_unit" title="Orbital replacement unit">orbital replacement units</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Five servicing missions (SM 1, 2, 3A, 3B, and 4) were flown by NASA <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_shuttle" title="Space shuttle">space shuttles</a>, the first in December 1993 and the last in May 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubble-timeline_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-hubble-timeline-77">[77]</a></sup> Servicing missions were delicate operations that began with maneuvering to intercept the telescope in orbit and carefully retrieving it with the shuttle's <a href="/wiki/Canadarm" title="Canadarm">mechanical arm</a>. The necessary work was then carried out in multiple tethered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spacewalk" title="Spacewalk">spacewalks</a> over a period of four to five days. After a visual inspection of the telescope, astronauts conducted repairs, replaced failed or degraded components, upgraded equipment, and installed new instruments. Once work was completed, the telescope was redeployed, typically after boosting to a higher orbit to address the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_decay" title="Orbital decay">orbital decay</a> caused by atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">drag</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes20150424_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes20150424-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Servicing_Mission_1">Servicing Mission 1</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Servicing Mission 1">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/STS-61" title="STS-61">STS-61</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Upgrading_Hubble_during_SM1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="986" data-file-width="992" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Upgrading_Hubble_during_SM1.jpg/220px-Upgrading_Hubble_during_SM1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Upgrading_Hubble_during_SM1.jpg/330px-Upgrading_Hubble_during_SM1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Upgrading_Hubble_during_SM1.jpg/440px-Upgrading_Hubble_during_SM1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Upgrading_Hubble_during_SM1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Astronauts Musgrave and Hoffman install corrective optics during SM1</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the problems with Hubble's mirror were discovered, the first servicing mission assumed greater importance, as the astronauts would need to do extensive work to install corrective optics. The seven astronauts for the mission were trained to use about a hundred specialized tools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> SM1 flew aboard <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour" title="Space Shuttle Endeavour"><i>Endeavour</i></a> in December 1993, and involved installation of several instruments and other equipment over ten days.</p>
<p>Most importantly, the <a href="/wiki/High_Speed_Photometer" title="High Speed Photometer">High Speed Photometer</a> was replaced with the <a href="/wiki/Corrective_Optics_Space_Telescope_Axial_Replacement" title="Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement">COSTAR</a> corrective optics package, and WFPC was replaced with the <a href="/wiki/Wide_Field_and_Planetary_Camera_2" title="Wide Field and Planetary Camera 2">Wide Field and Planetary Camera 2</a> (WFPC2) with an internal optical correction system. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_module" title="Photovoltaic module">solar arrays</a> and their drive electronics were also replaced, as well as four <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscopes</a> in the telescope pointing system, two electrical control units and other electrical components, and two magnetometers. The onboard computers were upgraded with added <a href="/wiki/Coprocessor" title="Coprocessor">coprocessors</a>, and Hubble's orbit was boosted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Servicing_Mission_1_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Servicing_Mission_1-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>On January 13, 1994, NASA declared the mission a complete success and showed the first sharper images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The mission was one of the most complex performed up until that date, involving five long <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra-vehicular_activity" title="Extra-vehicular activity">extra-vehicular activity</a> periods. Its success was a boon for NASA, as well as for the astronomers who now had a more capable space telescope.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Servicing_Mission_2">Servicing Mission 2</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Servicing Mission 2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/STS-82" title="STS-82">STS-82</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1536" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hubble_01.jpg/220px-Hubble_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hubble_01.jpg/330px-Hubble_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hubble_01.jpg/440px-Hubble_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hubble as seen from <i>Discovery</i> during its second servicing mission</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Servicing Mission 2, flown by <i>Discovery</i> in February 1997, replaced the GHRS and the FOS with the <a href="/wiki/Space_Telescope_Imaging_Spectrograph" title="Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph">Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph</a> (STIS) and the <a href="/wiki/Near_Infrared_Camera_and_Multi-Object_Spectrometer" title="Near Infrared Camera and Multi-Object Spectrometer">Near Infrared Camera and Multi-Object Spectrometer</a> (NICMOS), replaced an Engineering and Science Tape Recorder with a new Solid State Recorder, and repaired thermal insulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> NICMOS contained a <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat sink</a> of solid <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> to reduce the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_noise" title="Thermal noise">thermal noise</a> from the instrument, but shortly after it was installed, an unexpected <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a> resulted in part of the heat sink coming into contact with an optical baffle. This led to an increased warming rate for the instrument and reduced its original expected lifetime of 4.5 years to about 2 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICMOStemp_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-NICMOStemp-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Servicing_Mission_3A">Servicing Mission 3A</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Servicing Mission 3A">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/STS-103" title="STS-103">STS-103</a></div>
