<p>Servicing Mission 3A, flown by <i>Discovery</i>, took place in December 1999, and was a split-off from Servicing Mission 3 after three of the six onboard gyroscopes had failed. The fourth failed a few weeks before the mission, rendering the telescope incapable of performing scientific observations. The mission replaced all six gyroscopes, replaced a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fine_Guidance_Sensor" title="Fine Guidance Sensor">Fine Guidance Sensor</a> and the computer, installed a Voltage/temperature Improvement Kit (VIK) to prevent battery overcharging, and replaced thermal insulation blankets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The new computer is 20 times faster, with six times more memory, than the <a href="/wiki/DF-224" title="DF-224">DF-224</a> it replaced. It increases throughput by moving some computing tasks from the ground to the spacecraft and saves money by allowing the use of modern programming languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Servicing_Mission_3B">Servicing Mission 3B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Servicing Mission 3B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/STS-109" title="STS-109">STS-109</a></div>
<p>Servicing Mission 3B flown by <i>Columbia</i> in March 2002 saw the installation of a new instrument, with the FOC (which, except for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fine_Guidance_Sensor" title="Fine Guidance Sensor">Fine Guidance Sensors</a> when used for astrometry, was the last of the original instruments) being replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Camera_for_Surveys" title="Advanced Camera for Surveys">Advanced Camera for Surveys</a> (ACS). This meant that COSTAR was no longer required, since all new instruments had built-in correction for the main mirror aberration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COSTARNotNeeded_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-COSTARNotNeeded-74">[74]</a></sup> The mission also revived NICMOS by installing a closed-cycle cooler<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICMOStemp_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-NICMOStemp-82">[82]</a></sup> and replaced the solar arrays for the second time, providing 30 percent more power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Servicing_Mission_4">Servicing Mission 4</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Servicing Mission 4">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/STS-125" title="STS-125">STS-125</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS-125_May_17_EVA.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2843" data-file-width="4288" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/STS-125_May_17_EVA.jpg/200px-STS-125_May_17_EVA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/STS-125_May_17_EVA.jpg/300px-STS-125_May_17_EVA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/STS-125_May_17_EVA.jpg/400px-STS-125_May_17_EVA.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Hubble during SM4</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_telescope_2009.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2841" data-file-width="4288" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hubble_telescope_2009.jpg/200px-Hubble_telescope_2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hubble_telescope_2009.jpg/300px-Hubble_telescope_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hubble_telescope_2009.jpg/400px-Hubble_telescope_2009.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Hubble after release</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Plans called for Hubble to be serviced in February 2005, but the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbia_disaster" title="Columbia disaster"><i>Columbia</i> disaster</a> in 2003, in which the orbiter disintegrated on re-entry into the atmosphere, had wide-ranging effects on the Hubble program. NASA Administrator <a href="/wiki/Sean_O%27Keefe" title="Sean O'Keefe">Sean O'Keefe</a> decided that all future shuttle missions had to be able to reach the safe haven of the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> should in-flight problems develop. As no shuttles were capable of reaching both HST and the ISS during the same mission, future crewed service missions were canceled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> This decision was assailed by numerous astronomers, who felt that Hubble was valuable enough to merit the human risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> HST's planned successor, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Webb_Telescope" title="James Webb Telescope">James Webb Telescope</a> (JWST), was not expected to launch until at least 2018. A gap in space-observing capabilities between a decommissioning of Hubble and the commissioning of a successor was of major concern to many astronomers, given the significant scientific impact of HST.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The consideration that JWST will not be located in <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a>, and therefore cannot be easily upgraded or repaired in the event of an early failure, only makes these concerns more acute. On the other hand, many astronomers felt strongly that the servicing of Hubble should not take place if the expense were to come from the JWST budget.</p>
<p>In January 2004, O'Keefe said he would review his decision to cancel the final servicing mission to HST due to public outcry and requests from Congress for NASA to look for a way to save it. The National Academy of Sciences convened an official panel, which recommended in July 2004 that the HST should be preserved despite the apparent risks. Their report urged "NASA should take no actions that would preclude a space shuttle servicing mission to the Hubble Space Telescope".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> In August 2004, O'Keefe asked Goddard Space Flight Center to prepare a detailed proposal for a robotic service mission. These plans were later canceled, the robotic mission being described as "not feasible".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> In late 2004, several Congressional members, led by Senator <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Mikulski" title="Barbara Mikulski">Barbara Mikulski</a>, held public hearings and carried on a fight with much public support (including thousands of letters from school children across the country) to get the Bush Administration and NASA to reconsider the decision to drop plans for a Hubble rescue mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:218582main_Batt_FS_img1_lg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="567" data-file-width="886" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/218582main_Batt_FS_img1_lg.jpg/220px-218582main_Batt_FS_img1_lg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/218582main_Batt_FS_img1_lg.jpg/330px-218582main_Batt_FS_img1_lg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/218582main_Batt_FS_img1_lg.jpg/440px-218582main_Batt_FS_img1_lg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:218582main_Batt_FS_img1_lg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nickel–hydrogen battery pack for Hubble</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The nomination in April 2005 of a new NASA Administrator with an engineering rather than accounting background, <a href="/wiki/Michael_D._Griffin" title="Michael D. Griffin">Michael D. Griffin</a>, changed the situation, as Griffin stated he would consider a manned servicing mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_Light_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green_Light-92">[92]</a></sup> Soon after his appointment Griffin authorized Goddard to proceed with preparations for a manned Hubble maintenance flight, saying he would make the final decision after the next two shuttle missions. In October 2006 Griffin gave the final go-ahead, and the 11-day mission by <i>Atlantis</i> was scheduled for October 2008. Hubble's main data-handling unit failed in September 2008,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suddenly_quiet_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-suddenly_quiet-93">[93]</a></sup> halting all reporting of scientific data until its back-up was brought online on October 25, 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Since a failure of the backup unit would leave the HST helpless, the service mission was postponed to incorporate a replacement for the primary unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suddenly_quiet_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-suddenly_quiet-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Servicing Mission 4, flown by <i>Atlantis</i> in May 2009, was the last scheduled shuttle mission for HST.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SM4_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-SM4-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May09_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-May09-95">[95]</a></sup> SM4 installed the replacement data-handling unit, repaired the ACS and STIS systems, installed improved <a href="/wiki/Nickel%E2%80%93hydrogen_battery" title="Nickel–hydrogen battery">nickel hydrogen batteries</a>, and replaced other components. SM4 also installed two new observation instruments—<a href="/wiki/Wide_Field_Camera_3" title="Wide Field Camera 3">Wide Field Camera 3</a> (WFC3) and the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_Origins_Spectrograph" title="Cosmic Origins Spectrograph">Cosmic Origins Spectrograph</a> (COS)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HST_Opens_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-HST_Opens-96">[96]</a></sup>—and the Soft Capture and Rendezvous System, which will enable the future rendezvous, capture, and safe disposal of Hubble by either a crewed or robotic mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soft_Capture_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soft_Capture-97">[97]</a></sup> Except for the ACS's <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Camera_for_Surveys#High-Resolution_Channel_.28HRC.29" title="Advanced Camera for Surveys">High Resolution Channel</a> which could not be repaired and was disabled,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> the work accomplished during SM4 rendered the telescope fully functional,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SM4_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-SM4-75">[75]</a></sup> and it remains so as of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stsci-thisweek_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-stsci-thisweek-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_projects">Major projects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Major projects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pillars_of_creation_2014_HST_WFC3-UVIS_full-res_denoised.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7071" data-file-width="6780" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Pillars_of_creation_2014_HST_WFC3-UVIS_full-res_denoised.jpg/220px-Pillars_of_creation_2014_HST_WFC3-UVIS_full-res_denoised.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Pillars_of_creation_2014_HST_WFC3-UVIS_full-res_denoised.jpg/330px-Pillars_of_creation_2014_HST_WFC3-UVIS_full-res_denoised.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Pillars_of_creation_2014_HST_WFC3-UVIS_full-res_denoised.jpg/440px-Pillars_of_creation_2014_HST_WFC3-UVIS_full-res_denoised.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pillars_of_creation_2014_HST_WFC3-UVIS_full-res_denoised.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
One of Hubble's most famous images, <i><a href="/wiki/Pillars_of_Creation" title="Pillars of Creation">Pillars of Creation</a></i>, shows stars forming in the <a href="/wiki/Eagle_Nebula" title="Eagle Nebula">Eagle Nebula</a> (2014 image).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since the start of the program, a number of research projects have been carried out, some of them almost solely with Hubble, others coordinated facilities such as <a href="/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory" title="Chandra X-ray Observatory">Chandra X-ray Observatory</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESO" title="ESO">ESO</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope" title="Very Large Telescope">Very Large Telescope</a>. Although the Hubble observatory is nearing the end of its life, there are still major projects scheduled for it. One example is the upcoming Frontier Fields program,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SWGreport_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-SWGreport-102">[102]</a></sup> inspired by the results of Hubble's deep observation of the galaxy cluster <a href="/wiki/Abell_1689" title="Abell 1689">Abell 1689</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmic_Assembly_Near-infrared_Deep_Extragalactic_Legacy_Survey">Cosmic Assembly Near-infrared Deep Extragalactic Legacy Survey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Cosmic Assembly Near-infrared Deep Extragalactic Legacy Survey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In an August 2013 press release, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CANDELS" title="CANDELS">CANDELS</a> was referred to as "the largest project in the history of Hubble". The survey "aims to explore galactic evolution in the early Universe, and the very first seeds of cosmic structure at less than one billion years after the Big Bang."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The CANDELS project site describes the survey's goals as the following:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>The Cosmic Assembly Near-IR Deep Extragalactic Legacy Survey is designed to document the ﬁrst third of galactic evolution from z = 8 to 1.5 via deep imaging of more than 250,000 galaxies with WFC3/IR and ACS. It will also find the first Type Ia SNe beyond z &gt; 1.5 and establish their accuracy as standard candles for cosmology. Five premier multi-wavelength sky regions are selected; each has multi-wavelength data from Spitzer and other facilities, and has extensive spectroscopy of the brighter galaxies. The use of ﬁve widely separated ﬁelds mitigates cosmic variance and yields statistically robust and complete samples of galaxies down to 10<sup>9</sup> solar masses out to z ~ 8.</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Frontier_Fields_program">Frontier Fields program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Frontier Fields program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Color_image_of_galaxy_cluster_MCS_J0416.1%E2%80%932403.jpg"><img alt="Color image of galaxy cluster MCS J0416.1–2403, studied by the Hubble Frontier Fields program" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6484" data-file-width="6753" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Color_image_of_galaxy_cluster_MCS_J0416.1%E2%80%932403.jpg/220px-Color_image_of_galaxy_cluster_MCS_J0416.1%E2%80%932403.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Color_image_of_galaxy_cluster_MCS_J0416.1%E2%80%932403.jpg/330px-Color_image_of_galaxy_cluster_MCS_J0416.1%E2%80%932403.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Color_image_of_galaxy_cluster_MCS_J0416.1%E2%80%932403.jpg/440px-Color_image_of_galaxy_cluster_MCS_J0416.1%E2%80%932403.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Color_image_of_galaxy_cluster_MCS_J0416.1%E2%80%932403.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Frontier Fields program studied <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MACS0416.1-2403" title="MACS0416.1-2403">MACS0416.1-2403</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The program, officially named "Hubble Deep Fields Initiative 2012", is aimed to advance the knowledge of early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation" title="Galaxy formation">galaxy formation</a> by studying high-redshift galaxies in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_field" title="Deep field">blank fields</a> with the help of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_lensing" title="Gravitational lensing">gravitational lensing</a> to see the "faintest galaxies in the distant universe."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SWGreport_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-SWGreport-102">[102]</a></sup> The Frontier Fields web page describes the goals of the program being:</p>
<ul>
<li>to reveal hitherto inaccessible populations of z = 5–10 galaxies that are 10 to 50 times fainter intrinsically than any presently known</li>
<li>to solidify our understanding of the stellar masses and star formation histories of sub-L* galaxies at the earliest times</li>
<li>to provide the first statistically meaningful morphological characterization of star forming galaxies at z &gt; 5</li>
<li>to find z &gt; 8 galaxies stretched out enough by cluster lensing to discern internal structure and/or magnified enough by cluster lensing for spectroscopic follow-up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_use">Public use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Public use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EmissionNebula_NGC6357.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5057" data-file-width="3140" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/EmissionNebula_NGC6357.jpg/170px-EmissionNebula_NGC6357.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/EmissionNebula_NGC6357.jpg/255px-EmissionNebula_NGC6357.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/EmissionNebula_NGC6357.jpg/340px-EmissionNebula_NGC6357.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EmissionNebula_NGC6357.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Star cluster <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pismis_24" title="Pismis 24">Pismis 24</a> with <a href="/wiki/NGC_6357" title="NGC 6357">nebula</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Anyone can apply for time on the telescope; there are no restrictions on nationality or academic affiliation, but funding for analysis is only available to US institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Competition for time on the telescope is intense, with about one-fifth of the proposals submitted in each cycle earning time on the schedule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Calls for proposals are issued roughly annually, with time allocated for a cycle lasting about one year. Proposals are divided into several categories; "general observer" proposals are the most common, covering routine observations. "Snapshot observations" are those in which targets require only 45 minutes or less of telescope time, including overheads such as acquiring the target. Snapshot observations are used to fill in gaps in the telescope schedule that cannot be filled by regular GO programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>Astronomers may make "Target of Opportunity" proposals, in which observations are scheduled if a transient event covered by the proposal occurs during the scheduling cycle. In addition, up to 10% of the telescope time is designated "director's discretionary" (DD) time. Astronomers can apply to use DD time at any time of year, and it is typically awarded for study of unexpected transient phenomena such as supernovae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other uses of DD time have included the observations that led to views of the Hubble Deep Field and Hubble Ultra Deep Field, and in the first four cycles of telescope time, observations that were carried out by amateur astronomers.</p>
<p>Public imaging processing of Hubble data is encouraged as most of the data in the archives has not been processed into color imagery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubblesite.raiders_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-hubblesite.raiders-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amateur_observations">Amateur observations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Amateur observations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first director of STScI, <a href="/wiki/Riccardo_Giacconi" title="Riccardo Giacconi">Riccardo Giacconi</a>, announced in 1986 that he intended to devote some of his director discretionary time to allowing amateur astronomers to use the telescope. The total time to be allocated was only a few hours per cycle but excited great interest among amateur astronomers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Proposals for amateur time were stringently reviewed by a committee of amateur astronomers, and time was awarded only to proposals that were deemed to have genuine scientific merit, did not duplicate proposals made by professionals, and required the unique capabilities of the space telescope. Thirteen amateur astronomers were awarded time on the telescope, with observations being carried out between 1990 and 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Omeara1997_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Omeara1997-114">[114]</a></sup> One such study was "<a href="/wiki/Hubble_search_for_transition_comets" title="Hubble search for transition comets">Transition Comets—UV Search for OH</a>". The very first proposal, "A Hubble Space Telescope Study of Posteclipse Brightening and Albedo Changes on Io", was published in <i>Icarus</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> a journal devoted to solar system studies. A second study from another group of amateurs was also published in <i>Icarus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> After that time, however, budget reductions at STScI made the support of work by amateur astronomers untenable, and no additional amateur programs have been carried out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Omeara1997_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Omeara1997-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-motherboard20150424_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-motherboard20150424-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_results">Scientific results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Scientific results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Key_projects">Key projects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Key projects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the early 1980s, NASA and STScI convened four panels to discuss key projects. These were projects that were both scientifically important and would require significant telescope time, which would be explicitly dedicated to each project. This guaranteed that these particular projects would be completed early, in case the telescope failed sooner than expected. The panels identified three such projects: 1) a study of the nearby intergalactic medium using quasar absorption lines to determine the properties of the intergalactic medium and the gaseous content of galaxies and groups of galaxies;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> 2) a medium deep survey using the Wide Field Camera to take data whenever one of the other instruments was being used<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> and 3) a project to determine the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_constant" title="Hubble constant">Hubble constant</a> within ten percent by reducing the errors, both external and internal, in the calibration of the distance scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Important_discoveries">Important discoveries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Important discoveries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn.Aurora.HST.UV-Vis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2207" data-file-width="2131" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Saturn.Aurora.HST.UV-Vis.jpg/220px-Saturn.Aurora.HST.UV-Vis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Saturn.Aurora.HST.UV-Vis.jpg/330px-Saturn.Aurora.HST.UV-Vis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Saturn.Aurora.HST.UV-Vis.jpg/440px-Saturn.Aurora.HST.UV-Vis.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn.Aurora.HST.UV-Vis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hubble's STIS UV and ACS visible light are combined to reveal Saturn's southern aurora.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hubble has helped resolve some long-standing problems in astronomy, as well as raising new questions. Some results have required new <a href="/wiki/Scientific_theory" title="Scientific theory">theories</a> to explain them. Among its primary mission targets was to measure distances to <a href="/wiki/Cepheid_variable" title="Cepheid variable">Cepheid variable</a> stars more accurately than ever before, and thus <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law#Using_Hubble_space_telescope_data" title="Hubble's law">constrain the value</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_constant" title="Hubble constant">Hubble constant</a>, the measure of the rate at which the universe is expanding, which is also related to its age. Before the launch of HST, estimates of the Hubble constant typically had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Errors_and_residuals_in_statistics" title="Errors and residuals in statistics">errors</a> of up to 50%, but Hubble measurements of Cepheid variables in the <a href="/wiki/Virgo_Cluster" title="Virgo Cluster">Virgo Cluster</a> and other distant galaxy clusters provided a measured value with an accuracy of ±10%, which is consistent with other more accurate measurements made since Hubble's launch using other techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> The estimated age is now about 13.7 billion years, but before the Hubble Telescope scientists predicted an age ranging from 10 to 20 billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>While Hubble helped to refine estimates of the age of the universe, it also cast doubt on theories about its future. Astronomers from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-z_Supernova_Search_Team" title="High-z Supernova Search Team">High-z Supernova Search Team</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Supernova_Cosmology_Project" title="Supernova Cosmology Project">Supernova Cosmology Project</a> used ground-based telescopes and HST to observe distant <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a> and uncovered evidence that, far from decelerating under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, the expansion of the universe may in fact be <a href="/wiki/Deceleration_parameter" title="Deceleration parameter">accelerating</a>. Three members of these two groups have subsequently been awarded <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prizes</a> for their discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> The cause of this acceleration remains poorly understood;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> the most common cause attributed is <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="684" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg/220px-Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg/330px-Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg/440px-Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Brown spots mark <a href="/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker%E2%80%93Levy_9" title="Comet Shoemaker–Levy 9">Comet Shoemaker–Levy 9</a> impact sites on <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>'s southern hemisphere. Imaged by Hubble.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The high-resolution spectra and images provided by the HST have been especially well-suited to establishing the prevalence of <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a> in the nuclei of nearby galaxies. While it had been hypothesized in the early 1960s that black holes would be found at the centers of some galaxies, and astronomers in the 1980s identified a number of good black hole candidates, work conducted with Hubble shows that black holes are probably common to the centers of all galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> The Hubble programs further established that the masses of the nuclear black holes and properties of the galaxies are closely related. The legacy of the Hubble programs on black holes in galaxies is thus to demonstrate a deep connection between galaxies and their central black holes.</p>
<p>The collision of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker-Levy_9" title="Comet Shoemaker-Levy 9">Comet Shoemaker-Levy 9</a> with <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> in 1994 was fortuitously timed for astronomers, coming just a few months after Servicing Mission 1 had restored Hubble's optical performance. Hubble images of the <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> were sharper than any taken since the passage of <a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a> in 1979, and were crucial in studying the dynamics of the collision of a comet with Jupiter, an event believed to occur once every few centuries.</p>
<p>Other discoveries made with Hubble data include proto-planetary disks (<a href="/wiki/Proplyd" title="Proplyd">proplyds</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/Orion_Nebula" title="Orion Nebula">Orion Nebula</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> evidence for the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">extrasolar planets</a> around Sun-like stars;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> and the optical counterparts of the still-mysterious <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_ray_burst" title="Gamma ray burst">gamma ray bursts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> HST has also been used to study objects in the outer reaches of the Solar System, including the dwarf planets <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_(full_resolution).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2078" data-file-width="2382" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_%28full_resolution%29.png/220px-Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_%28full_resolution%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_%28full_resolution%29.png/330px-Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_%28full_resolution%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_%28full_resolution%29.png/440px-Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_%28full_resolution%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_(full_resolution).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hubble Extreme Deep Field image of space in the constellation <a href="/wiki/Fornax" title="Fornax">Fornax</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A unique window on the Universe enabled by Hubble are the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Deep Field">Hubble Deep Field</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Ultra-Deep_Field" title="Hubble Ultra-Deep Field">Hubble Ultra-Deep Field</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Extreme Deep Field">Hubble Extreme Deep Field</a> images, which used Hubble's unmatched sensitivity at visible wavelengths to create images of small patches of sky that are the deepest ever obtained at optical wavelengths. The images reveal galaxies billions of light years away, and have generated a wealth of scientific papers, providing a new window on the early Universe. The Wide Field Camera 3 improved the view of these fields in the infrared and ultraviolet, supporting the discovery of some of the most distant objects yet discovered, such as <a href="/wiki/MACS0647-JD" title="MACS0647-JD">MACS0647-JD</a>.</p>
<p>The non-standard object <a href="/wiki/SCP_06F6" title="SCP 06F6">SCP 06F6</a> was discovered by the Hubble Space Telescope in February 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gans_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-gans-135">[135]</a></sup> During June and July 2012, US astronomers using Hubble discovered a tiny fifth moon moving around icy Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2015, researchers announced that measurements of aurorae around <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a> revealed that the moon has a subsurface ocean. Using Hubble to study the motion of its aurorae, the researchers determined that a large saltwater ocean was helping to suppress the interaction between Jupiter's magnetic field and that of Ganymede. The ocean is estimated to be 100 km (60 mi) deep, trapped beneath a 150 km (90 mi) ice crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stsci20150312_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-stsci20150312-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-search_ocean_Ganymede_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-search_ocean_Ganymede-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>On December 11, 2015, Hubble captured an image of the first-ever predicted reappearance of a <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a>, dubbed "<a href="/wiki/SN_Refsdal" title="SN Refsdal">Refsdal</a>", which was calculated using different mass models of a galaxy cluster whose gravity is <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">warping</a> the supernova's light. The supernova was previously seen in November 2014 behind galaxy cluster <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=MACS_J1149.5%2B2223&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="MACS J1149.5+2223 (page does not exist)">MACS J1149.5+2223</a> as part of Hubble's Frontier Fields program. Astronomers spotted four separate images of the supernova in an arrangement known as an <a href="/wiki/Einstein_Cross" title="Einstein Cross">Einstein Cross</a>. The light from the cluster has taken about five billion years to reach Earth, though the supernova exploded some 10 billion years ago. The detection of Refsdal's reappearance served as a unique opportunity for astronomers to test their models of how mass, especially <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>, is distributed within this galaxy cluster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 3, 2016, researchers using Hubble data announced the discovery of the farthest known galaxy to date: <a href="/wiki/GN-z11" title="GN-z11">GN-z11</a>. The Hubble observations occurred on February 11, 2015, and April 3, 2015, as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CANDELS" title="CANDELS">CANDELS</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GOODS" title="GOODS">GOODS</a>-North surveys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_on_astronomy">Impact on astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Impact on astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_Probes_the_Early_Universe.jpg"><img alt="Depiction of progress in the detection of the early Universe" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="559" data-file-width="1050" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Hubble_Probes_the_Early_Universe.jpg/260px-Hubble_Probes_the_Early_Universe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Hubble_Probes_the_Early_Universe.jpg/390px-Hubble_Probes_the_Early_Universe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Hubble_Probes_the_Early_Universe.jpg/520px-Hubble_Probes_the_Early_Universe.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_Probes_the_Early_Universe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Evolution of detecting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Big_Bang" title="Timeline of the Big Bang">early Universe</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Many objective measures show the positive impact of Hubble data on astronomy. Over 9,000 papers based on Hubble data have been published in peer-reviewed journals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> and countless more have appeared in conference <a href="/wiki/Proceedings" title="Proceedings">proceedings</a>. Looking at papers several years after their publication, about one-third of all astronomy papers have no <a href="/wiki/Citation" title="Citation">citations</a>, while only 2% of papers based on Hubble data have no citations. On average, a paper based on Hubble data receives about twice as many citations as papers based on non-Hubble data. Of the 200 papers published each year that receive the most citations, about 10% are based on Hubble data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the HST has clearly helped astronomical research, its financial cost has been large. A study on the relative astronomical benefits of different sizes of telescopes found that while papers based on HST data generate 15 times as many citations as a 4 m (13 ft) ground-based telescope such as the <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel_Telescope" title="William Herschel Telescope">William Herschel Telescope</a>, the HST costs about 100 times as much to build and maintain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>Deciding between building ground- versus space-based telescopes is complex. Even before Hubble was launched, specialized ground-based techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Aperture_masking_interferometry" title="Aperture masking interferometry">aperture masking interferometry</a> had obtained higher-resolution optical and infrared images than Hubble would achieve, though restricted to targets about 10<sup>8</sup> times brighter than the faintest targets observed by Hubble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Since then, advances in <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_optics" title="Adaptive optics">adaptive optics</a> have extended the high-resolution imaging capabilities of ground-based telescopes to the infrared imaging of faint objects. The usefulness of adaptive optics versus HST observations depends strongly on the particular details of the research questions being asked. In the visible bands, adaptive optics can only correct a relatively small field of view, whereas HST can conduct high-resolution optical imaging over a wide field. Only a small fraction of astronomical objects are accessible to high-resolution ground-based imaging; in contrast Hubble can perform high-resolution observations of any part of the night sky, and on objects that are extremely faint.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aerospace_engineering">Aerospace engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Aerospace engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In addition to its scientific results, Hubble has also made significant contributions to <a href="/wiki/Aerospace_engineering" title="Aerospace engineering">aerospace engineering</a>, in particular the performance of systems in low Earth orbit. These insights result from Hubble's long lifetime on orbit, extensive instrumentation, and return of assemblies to the Earth where they can be studied in detail. In particular, Hubble has contributed to studies of the behavior of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fiber_reinforced_polymer" title="Carbon fiber reinforced polymer">graphite composite</a> structures in vacuum, optical contamination from residual gas and human servicing, <a href="/wiki/Radiation_damage" title="Radiation damage">radiation damage</a> to electronics and sensors, and the long term behavior of <a href="/wiki/Multi-layer_insulation" title="Multi-layer insulation">multi-layer insulation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> One lesson learned was that gyros assembled using pressurized oxygen to deliver suspension fluid were prone to failure due to electric wire corrosion. Gyros are now assembled using pressurized nitrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gyros_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gyros-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hubble_data">Hubble data</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Hubble data">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_stretches_the_stellar_tape_measure_ten_times_further.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3150" data-file-width="2400" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Hubble_stretches_the_stellar_tape_measure_ten_times_further.jpg/220px-Hubble_stretches_the_stellar_tape_measure_ten_times_further.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Hubble_stretches_the_stellar_tape_measure_ten_times_further.jpg/330px-Hubble_stretches_the_stellar_tape_measure_ten_times_further.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Hubble_stretches_the_stellar_tape_measure_ten_times_further.jpg/440px-Hubble_stretches_the_stellar_tape_measure_ten_times_further.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_stretches_the_stellar_tape_measure_ten_times_further.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hubble precision stellar distance measurement has been extended 10 times further into the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission_to_Earth">Transmission to Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Transmission to Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hubble data was initially stored on the spacecraft. When launched, the storage facilities were old-fashioned reel-to-reel <a href="/wiki/Tape_recorder" title="Tape recorder">tape recorders</a>, but these were replaced by <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_drive" title="Solid-state drive">solid state</a> data storage facilities during servicing missions 2 and 3A. About twice daily, the Hubble Space Telescope radios data to a satellite in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geosynchronous" title="Geosynchronous">geosynchronous</a> <a href="/wiki/Tracking_and_Data_Relay_Satellite_System" title="Tracking and Data Relay Satellite System">Tracking and Data Relay Satellite System</a> (TDRSS), which then downlinks the science data to one of two 60-foot (18-meter) diameter high-gain microwave antennas located at the <a href="/wiki/White_Sands_Test_Facility" title="White Sands Test Facility">White Sands Test Facility</a> in <a href="/wiki/White_Sands,_New_Mexico" title="White Sands, New Mexico">White Sands, New Mexico</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telemetry_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Telemetry-150">[150]</a></sup> From there they are sent to the Space Telescope Operations Control Center at Goddard Space Flight Center, and finally to the Space Telescope Science Institute for archiving.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telemetry_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-Telemetry-150">[150]</a></sup> Each week, HST downlinks approximately 140 gigabits of data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubblesite-facts_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-hubblesite-facts-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Color_images">Color images</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Color images">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cumulative-absorption-spectrum-hubble-telescope.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Cumulative-absorption-spectrum-hubble-telescope.jpg/220px-Cumulative-absorption-spectrum-hubble-telescope.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Cumulative-absorption-spectrum-hubble-telescope.jpg/330px-Cumulative-absorption-spectrum-hubble-telescope.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Cumulative-absorption-spectrum-hubble-telescope.jpg/440px-Cumulative-absorption-spectrum-hubble-telescope.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cumulative-absorption-spectrum-hubble-telescope.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Data analysis of a spectrum reveals the chemistry of hidden clouds</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All images from Hubble are <a href="/wiki/Monochrome" title="Monochrome">monochromatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Grayscale" title="Grayscale">grayscale</a>, in which its cameras incorporate a variety of filters each passing specific wavelengths of light. Color images are created by combining separate monochrome images taken through different filters. This process can also create <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/False-color" title="False-color">false-color</a> versions of images including infrared and ultraviolet channels, where infrared is typically rendered as a deep red and ultraviolet is rendered as a deep blue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space20130724_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-space20130724-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archives">Archives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Archives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All Hubble data is eventually made available via the <a href="/wiki/Space_Telescope_Science_Institute" title="Space Telescope Science Institute">Mikulski Archive for Space Telescopes</a> at <a href="/wiki/Space_Telescope_Science_Institute" title="Space Telescope Science Institute">STScI</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_Astronomy_Data_Centre" title="Canadian Astronomy Data Centre">CADC</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Astronomy_Centre" title="European Space Astronomy Centre">ESA/ESAC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> Data is usually proprietary—available only to the <a href="/wiki/Principal_investigator" title="Principal investigator">principal investigator</a> (PI) and astronomers designated by the PI—for one year after being taken. The PI can apply to the director of the STScI to extend or reduce the proprietary period in some circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>Observations made on Director's Discretionary Time are exempt from the proprietary period, and are released to the public immediately. Calibration data such as flat fields and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dark_frame" title="Dark frame">dark frames</a> are also publicly available straight away. All data in the archive is in the <a href="/wiki/FITS" title="FITS">FITS</a> format, which is suitable for astronomical analysis but not for public use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Heritage_Project" title="Hubble Heritage Project">Hubble Heritage Project</a> processes and releases to the public a small selection of the most striking images in <a href="/wiki/JPEG" title="JPEG">JPEG</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tagged_Image_File_Format" title="Tagged Image File Format">TIFF</a> formats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heritage.stsci_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-heritage.stsci-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pipeline_reduction">Pipeline reduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Pipeline reduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Astronomical data taken with <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCDs</a> must undergo several calibration steps before they are suitable for astronomical analysis. STScI has developed sophisticated software that automatically calibrates data when they are requested from the archive using the best calibration files available. This 'on-the-fly' processing means that large data requests can take a day or more to be processed and returned. The process by which data is calibrated automatically is known as 'pipeline reduction', and is increasingly common at major observatories. Astronomers may if they wish retrieve the calibration files themselves and run the pipeline reduction software locally. This may be desirable when calibration files other than those selected automatically need to be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Data_analysis">Data analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Data analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hubble data can be analyzed using many different packages. STScI maintains the custom-made Space Telescope Science Data Analysis System (STSDAS) software, which contains all the programs needed to run pipeline reduction on raw data files, as well as many other astronomical image processing tools, tailored to the requirements of Hubble data. The software runs as a module of <a href="/wiki/IRAF" title="IRAF">IRAF</a>, a popular astronomical data reduction program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Outreach_activities">Outreach activities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Outreach activities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>It has always been important for the Space Telescope to capture the public's imagination, given the considerable contribution of <a href="/wiki/Taxpayer" title="Taxpayer">taxpayers</a> to its construction and operational costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> After the difficult early years when the faulty mirror severely dented Hubble's reputation with the public, the first servicing mission allowed its rehabilitation as the corrected optics produced numerous remarkable images.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Horsehead-Hubble.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2065" data-file-width="2839" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Horsehead-Hubble.jpg/220px-Horsehead-Hubble.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Horsehead-Hubble.jpg/330px-Horsehead-Hubble.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Horsehead-Hubble.jpg/440px-Horsehead-Hubble.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Horsehead-Hubble.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In 2001, NASA polled internet users to find out what they would most like Hubble to observe; they overwhelmingly selected the <a href="/wiki/Horsehead_Nebula" title="Horsehead Nebula">Horsehead Nebula</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Several initiatives have helped to keep the public informed about Hubble activities. In the United States, <a href="/wiki/Outreach" title="Outreach">outreach</a> efforts are coordinated by the Space Telescope Science Institute (STScI) Office for Public Outreach, which was established in 2000 to ensure that U.S. taxpayers saw the benefits of their investment in the space telescope program. To that end, STScI operates the HubbleSite.org website. The <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Heritage_Project" title="Hubble Heritage Project">Hubble Heritage Project</a>, operating out of the STScI, provides the public with high-quality images of the most interesting and striking objects observed. The Heritage team is composed of amateur and professional astronomers, as well as people with backgrounds outside astronomy, and emphasizes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aesthetic" title="Aesthetic">aesthetic</a> nature of Hubble images. The Heritage Project is granted a small amount of time to observe objects which, for scientific reasons, may not have images taken at enough wavelengths to construct a full-color image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heritage.stsci_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-heritage.stsci-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="Hubblecast"></span>Since 1999, the leading Hubble outreach group in Europe has been the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_European_Space_Agency_Information_Centre" title="Hubble European Space Agency Information Centre">Hubble European Space Agency Information Centre</a> (HEIC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> This office was established at the <a href="/wiki/Space_Telescope_European_Coordinating_Facility" title="Space Telescope European Coordinating Facility">Space Telescope European Coordinating Facility</a> in Munich, Germany. HEIC's mission is to fulfill HST outreach and education tasks for the European Space Agency. The work is centered on the production of news and photo releases that highlight interesting Hubble results and images. These are often European in origin, and so increase awareness of both ESA's Hubble share (15%) and the contribution of European scientists to the observatory. ESA produces educational material, including a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Videocast" title="Videocast">videocast</a> series called Hubblecast designed to share world-class scientific news with the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Hubble Space Telescope has won two Space Achievement Awards from the <a href="/wiki/Space_Foundation" title="Space Foundation">Space Foundation</a>, for its outreach activities, in 2001 and 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is a replica of the Hubble Space Telescope on the courthouse lawn in <a href="/wiki/Marshfield,_Missouri" title="Marshfield, Missouri">Marshfield, Missouri</a>, the hometown of namesake Edwin P. Hubble.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Celebration_images">Celebration images</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Celebration images">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Hubble_anniversary_images" title="List of Hubble anniversary images">List of Hubble anniversary images</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HH_901_and_HH_902_in_the_Carina_nebula_(captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1937" data-file-width="2104" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/HH_901_and_HH_902_in_the_Carina_nebula_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.jpg/220px-HH_901_and_HH_902_in_the_Carina_nebula_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/HH_901_and_HH_902_in_the_Carina_nebula_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.jpg/330px-HH_901_and_HH_902_in_the_Carina_nebula_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/HH_901_and_HH_902_in_the_Carina_nebula_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.jpg/440px-HH_901_and_HH_902_in_the_Carina_nebula_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HH_901_and_HH_902_in_the_Carina_nebula_(captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A pillar of gas and dust in the <a href="/wiki/Carina_Nebula" title="Carina Nebula">Carina Nebula</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Wide_Field_Camera_3" title="Wide Field Camera 3">Wide Field Camera 3</a> image, dubbed <i><a href="/wiki/Mystic_Mountain" title="Mystic Mountain">Mystic Mountain</a></i>, was released in 2010 to commemorate Hubble's 20th anniversary in space.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Hubble Space Telescope celebrated its 20th anniversary in space on April 24, 2010. To commemorate the occasion, NASA, ESA, and the Space Telescope Science Institute (STScI) released an image from the <a href="/wiki/Carina_Nebula" title="Carina Nebula">Carina Nebula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>To commemorate Hubble's 25th anniversary in space on April 24, 2015, STScI released images of the <a href="/wiki/Westerlund_2" title="Westerlund 2">Westerlund 2</a> cluster, located about 20,000 light-years (6,100 pc) away in the constellation Carina, through its Hubble 25 website.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> The European Space Agency created a dedicated 25th anniversary page on its website.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> In April 2016, a special celebratory image of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bubble_Nebula" title="Bubble Nebula">Bubble Nebula</a> was released for Hubble's 26th "birthday".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Equipment_failures">Equipment failures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Equipment failures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Formalhaut_b.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3000" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Formalhaut_b.jpg/220px-Formalhaut_b.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Formalhaut_b.jpg/330px-Formalhaut_b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Formalhaut_b.jpg/440px-Formalhaut_b.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Formalhaut_b.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hubble views the <a href="/wiki/Fomalhaut" title="Fomalhaut">Fomalhaut</a> system, imaged with the offline ACS/HRC channel</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Past servicing missions have exchanged old instruments for new ones, both avoiding failure and making possible new types of science. Without servicing missions, all of the instruments will eventually fail. In August 2004, the power system of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Telescope_Imaging_Spectrograph" title="Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph">Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph</a> (STIS) failed, rendering the instrument inoperable. The electronics had originally been fully redundant, but the first set of electronics failed in May 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> This power supply was fixed during Servicing Mission 4 in May 2009.</p>
<p>Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Camera_for_Surveys" title="Advanced Camera for Surveys">Advanced Camera for Surveys</a> (ACS) main camera primary electronics failed in June 2006, and the power supply for the backup electronics failed on January 27, 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> Only the instrument's Solar Blind Channel (SBC) was operable using the side-1 electronics. A new power supply for the wide angle channel was added during SM 4, but quick tests revealed this did not help the high resolution channel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> The Wide Field Channel (WFC) was returned to service by <a href="/wiki/STS-125" title="STS-125">STS-125</a> in May 2009 but the High Resolution Channel (HRC) remains offline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>HST uses <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscopes</a> to detect and measure any rotations so it can stabilize itself in orbit and point accurately and steadily at astronomical targets. Normally, three gyroscopes are required for operation; observations are still possible with two, but the area of sky that can be viewed would be somewhat restricted, and observations requiring very accurate pointing are more difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> There are further contingency plans for observations with just one gyro,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> but if all gyros fail, continued scientific observations will not be possible. In 2005, it was decided to switch to two-gyroscope mode for regular telescope operations as a means of extending the lifetime of the mission. The switch to this mode was made in August 2005, leaving Hubble with two gyroscopes in use, two on backup, and two inoperable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> One more gyro failed in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> By the time of the final repair mission in May 2009, during which all six gyros were replaced (with two new pairs and one refurbished pair), only three gyros were still working. Engineers determined that the gyro failures were caused by corrosion of electric wires powering the motor that was initiated by oxygen-pressurized air used to deliver the thick suspending fluid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gyros_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gyros-148">[148]</a></sup> The new gyro models were assembled using pressurized nitrogen<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gyros_148-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gyros-148">[148]</a></sup> and they should be much more reliable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Future">Future</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Future">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_decay_and_controlled_reentry">Orbital decay and controlled reentry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Orbital decay and controlled reentry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soft_Capture_Mechanism_installed_on_Hubble_(illustration).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="780" data-file-width="1300" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Soft_Capture_Mechanism_installed_on_Hubble_%28illustration%29.jpg/220px-Soft_Capture_Mechanism_installed_on_Hubble_%28illustration%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Soft_Capture_Mechanism_installed_on_Hubble_%28illustration%29.jpg/330px-Soft_Capture_Mechanism_installed_on_Hubble_%28illustration%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Soft_Capture_Mechanism_installed_on_Hubble_%28illustration%29.jpg/440px-Soft_Capture_Mechanism_installed_on_Hubble_%28illustration%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soft_Capture_Mechanism_installed_on_Hubble_(illustration).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illustration of the Soft Capture Mechanism (SCM) installed on Hubble</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hubble orbits the Earth in the extremely tenuous upper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, and over time its orbit <a href="/wiki/Orbital_decay" title="Orbital decay">decays</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">drag</a>. If it is not re-boosted, it will re-enter the Earth's atmosphere within some decades, with the exact date depending on how active the Sun is and its impact on the upper atmosphere. If Hubble were to descend in a completely uncontrolled re-entry, parts of the main mirror and its support structure would probably survive, leaving the potential for damage or even human fatalities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> In 2013, deputy project manager James Jeletic projected that Hubble could survive into 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbsnews20130530_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-cbsnews20130530-3">[3]</a></sup> Based on solar activity and atmospheric drag, or lack thereof, a natural atmospheric reentry for Hubble will occur between 2028 and 2040.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbsnews20130530_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-cbsnews20130530-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> In June 2016, NASA extended the service contract for Hubble until June 2021.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>NASA's original plan for safely de-orbiting Hubble was to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/STS-144" title="STS-144">retrieve it using a space shuttle</a>. Hubble would then have most likely been displayed in the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>. This is no longer possible since the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_retirement" title="Space Shuttle retirement">space shuttle fleet has been retired</a>, and would have been unlikely in any case due to the cost of the mission and risk to the crew. Instead, NASA considered adding an external propulsion module to allow controlled re-entry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> Ultimately NASA installed the Soft Capture and Rendezvous System, to enable deorbit by either a crewed or robotic mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soft_Capture_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Soft_Capture-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_commercial_service_missions">Possible commercial service missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Possible commercial service missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trump_Administration" title="Trump Administration">Trump Administration</a> is considering a proposal by the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_Corporation" title="Sierra Nevada Corporation">Sierra Nevada Corporation</a> to use a manned version of its <a href="/wiki/Dream_Chaser" title="Dream Chaser">Dream Chaser</a> spacecraft to service <i>Hubble</i> some time in the 2020s both as a continuation of its scientific capabilities and as insurance against any malfunctions in the to-be-launched <a href="/wiki/James_Webb_Space_Telescope" title="James Webb Space Telescope">James Webb Space Telescope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Popular_Mechanics-2017-02-03_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Popular_Mechanics-2017-02-03-183">[183]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Successors">Successors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Successors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/James_Webb_Space_Telescope" title="James Webb Space Telescope">James Webb Space Telescope</a></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; font-size:11px; float:right; margin:10px">
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;"><b> <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">Visible spectrum range</a> </b></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Color" title="Color">Color</a></th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">Wavelength</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="background:#ccb0f4;"><b><a href="/wiki/Violet_(color)" title="Violet (color)">violet</a></b></td>
<td>380–450 nm</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="background:#b0b0f4;"><b><a href="/wiki/Blue" title="Blue">blue</a></b></td>
<td>450–475 nm</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="background:#b0f4f4;"><b><a href="/wiki/Cyan" title="Cyan">cyan</a></b></td>
<td>476–495 nm</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="background:#b0f4b0;"><b><a href="/wiki/Green" title="Green">green</a></b></td>
<td>495–570 nm</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="background:#f4f4b0;"><b><a href="/wiki/Yellow" title="Yellow">yellow</a></b></td>
<td>570–590 nm</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="background:#f4ccb0;"><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orange_(color)" title="Orange (color)">orange</a></b></td>
<td>590–620 nm</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="background:#f4b0b0;"><b><a href="/wiki/Red" title="Red">red</a></b></td>
<td>620–750 nm</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>There is no direct replacement to Hubble as an ultraviolet and visible light space telescope, because near-term space telescopes do not duplicate Hubble's wavelength coverage (near-ultraviolet to near-infrared wavelengths), instead concentrating on the farther infrared bands. These bands are preferred for studying high redshift and low-temperature objects, objects generally older and farther away in the universe. These wavelengths are also difficult or impossible to study from the ground, justifying the expense of a space-based telescope. Large ground-based telescopes can image some of the same wavelengths as Hubble, sometimes challenge HST in terms of resolution by using <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_optics" title="Adaptive optics">adaptive optics</a> (AO), have much larger light-gathering power, and can be upgraded more easily, but cannot yet match Hubble's excellent resolution over a wide field of view with the very dark background of space.</p>
<p>Plans for a Hubble successor materialized as the Next Generation Space Telescope project, which culminated in plans for the <a href="/wiki/James_Webb_Space_Telescope" title="James Webb Space Telescope">James Webb Space Telescope</a> (JWST), the formal successor of Hubble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SAOverview_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-SAOverview-184">[184]</a></sup> Very different from a scaled-up Hubble, it is designed to operate colder and farther away from the Earth at the L2 <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian point</a>, where thermal and optical interference from the Earth and Moon are lessened. It is not engineered to be fully serviceable (such as replaceable instruments), but the design includes a docking ring to enable visits from other spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> A main scientific goal of JWST is to observe the most distant objects in the universe, beyond the reach of existing instruments. It is expected to detect stars in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Big_Bang" title="Timeline of the Big Bang">early Universe</a> approximately 280 million years older than stars HST now detects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> The telescope is an international collaboration between NASA, the European Space Agency, and the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Space_Agency" title="Canadian Space Agency">Canadian Space Agency</a> since 1996,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> and is planned for launch on an <a href="/wiki/Ariane_5" title="Ariane 5">Ariane 5</a> rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Although JWST is primarily an infrared instrument, its coverage extends down to 600 nm wavelength light, or roughly orange in the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible spectrum</a>. A typical human eye can see to about 750 nm wavelength light, so there is some overlap with the longest visible wavelength bands, including orange and red light.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
