<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JWST-HST-primary-mirrors.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="957" data-file-width="1414" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/JWST-HST-primary-mirrors.svg/220px-JWST-HST-primary-mirrors.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/JWST-HST-primary-mirrors.svg/330px-JWST-HST-primary-mirrors.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/JWST-HST-primary-mirrors.svg/440px-JWST-HST-primary-mirrors.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JWST-HST-primary-mirrors.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hubble and JWST mirrors (4.5 m<sup>2</sup> and 25 m<sup>2</sup> respectively)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A complementary telescope, looking at even longer wavelengths than Hubble or JWST, was the European Space Agency's <a href="/wiki/Herschel_Space_Observatory" title="Herschel Space Observatory">Herschel Space Observatory</a>, launched on May 14, 2009. Like JWST, Herschel was not designed to be serviced after launch, and had a mirror substantially larger than Hubble's, but observed only in the <a href="/wiki/Far_infrared" title="Far infrared">far infrared</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Submillimeter_astronomy" title="Submillimeter astronomy">submillimeter</a>. It needed helium coolant, of which it ran out on April 29, 2013.</p>

<p>Further concepts for advanced 21st-century space telescopes include the <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Technology_Large-Aperture_Space_Telescope" title="Advanced Technology Large-Aperture Space Telescope">Advanced Technology Large-Aperture Space Telescope</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> a conceptualized 8- to 16-meter (320- to 640-inch) optical space telescope that if realized could be a more direct successor to HST, with the ability to observe and photograph astronomical objects in the visible, ultraviolet, and infrared wavelengths, with substantially better resolution than Hubble or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_telescope" title="Spitzer Space telescope">Spitzer Space telescope</a>. This effort is being planned for the 2025–2035 time frame.</p>
<p>Existing ground-based telescopes, and various proposed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extremely_Large_Telescopes" title="Extremely Large Telescopes">Extremely Large Telescopes</a>, can exceed the HST in terms of sheer light-gathering power and diffraction limit due to larger mirrors, but other factors affect telescopes. In some cases, they may be able to match or beat Hubble in resolution by using adaptive optics. However, AO on large ground-based reflectors will not make Hubble and other space telescopes obsolete. Most AO systems sharpen the view over a very narrow field—<a href="/wiki/Lucky_imaging" title="Lucky imaging">Lucky Cam</a>, for example, produces crisp images just 10" to 20" wide, whereas Hubble's cameras produce crisp images across a 2½' (150") field. Furthermore, space telescopes can study the universe across the entire electromagnetic spectrum, most of which is blocked by Earth's atmosphere. Finally, the background sky is darker in space than on the ground, because air absorbs solar energy during the day and then releases it at night, producing a faint—but nevertheless discernible—<a href="/wiki/Airglow" title="Airglow">airglow</a> that washes out low-contrast astronomical objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fienberg_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fienberg-191">[191]</a></sup></p>

"Hubble repair improves vision and helps restore NASA's image". <i>Physics Today</i>. <b>47</b> (3): 42. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1994PhT....47c..42G" rel="nofollow">1994PhT....47c..42G</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1063%2F1.2808434" rel="nofollow">10.1063/1.2808434</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Physics+Today&amp;rft.atitle=Hubble+repair+improves+vision+and+helps+restore+NASA%27s+image&amp;rft.volume=47&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=42&amp;rft.date=1994&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1063%2F1.2808434&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1994PhT....47c..42G&amp;rft.au=Goodwin%2C+Irwin&amp;rft.au=Cioffi%2C+Denis+F.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHubble+Space+Telescope"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dunar, pp. 514–515.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Allen, Chapter VII. The spacing of the field lens in the corrector was to have been done by laser measurements off the end of an invar bar. Instead of illuminating the end of the bar, however, the laser in fact was reflected from a worn spot on a black-anodized metal cap placed over the end of the bar to isolate its center (visible through a hole in the cap). The technician who performed the test noted an unexpected gap between the field lens and its supporting structure in the corrector and filled it in with an ordinary metal washer.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dunar, p. 512: "the firm’s optical operations personnel dismissed the evidence as itself flawed. They believed the other two null correctors were less accurate than the reflective null corrector and so could not verify its reliability. Since they assumed the perfection of the mirror and reflective null corrector, they rejected falsifying information from independent tests, believed no problems existed, and reported only good news."</span></li>
